Unseen Matters
beginner
5 min
Certainty about Unseen Matters through Revelation
Explains that unseen matters require detailed revelation because reason cannot reach them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unseen matters cannot be known through empirical reason and how the Qur'an and mutawatir Sunnah provide certainty via tawatur.

Core Idea
Unseen matters require revealed certainty; tawatur makes that certainty rigorous.

In this passage, the author establishes a foundational principle of Islamic epistemology: the realm of the unseen (al-ghayb) is inaccessible to human reason and observation. Therefore, certainty about unseen matters must come from divine revelation transmitted through an unbroken chain of reliable narrators (tawatur). Let's see how he develops this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: reason cannot reach unseen matters
Read the first sentence. Note that the Qur'an treats unseen reports with a decisive judgement because experiment and observation cannot reach them.
This sets the stage for why revelation is necessary.
2
Find the two sources of certainty
The passage states that certainty about unseen matters comes only from the news of God (Qur'an) or the mutawatir Sunnah. Underline this.
This is the key claim.
3
Understand the division of unseen claims
Some have an explicit text in the primary sources, some do not. Those with an explicit text become certain perceptions.
The author limits certainty to explicitly revealed matters.
4
Trace the justification: tawatur
The reason these texts yield certainty is that they are transmitted via tawatur (mass transmission). The Qur'an and mutawatir Sunnah are both transmitted in this way.
This connects to the earlier discussion on tawatur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما مافيه من الإخبارات الغيبية فإنه قد فصل القول فيها بحكم مبرم ، لأنه لا مطمع عن طريق شيء من التجربة والمشاهدة في الوصول إليها . وليس السبيل إلى اليقين بها إلا خبر الله جل جلاله أو السنة المتواترة هذا فيما يتعلق بأمور محسوسة • وأما ماكان من الدعاوى متعلقاً بأمر تجريدي أو غيبي غير خاضع لشيء من الحواس الظاهرة ، فمنه ماتجد في الكتاب أو متواتر السنة نصاً واضحاً فيه ، ومنه ما لا تجد في شيء منها حديثاً واضحاً عنه · فأما المنصوص عليه في أحدهما : فهو داخل بذلك في المدركات اليقينية . وسبيل اليقين به أنه من حيث نقل الكتاب أو السنة له ، يرجع إلى الخبر اليقيني المتواتر الذي فرغنا من البحث فيه . إذ القرآن إنما هو اللفظ الموحى به إلى صل عروسة والواصل إلينا عن طريق التواتر ، فلا جرم أن قرآنية ألفاظه مقطوع بها . ومثل القرآن في ذلك السنة إذا وصلت إلينا متواترة .
As for the unseen reports contained in it, it has detailed them with a decisive judgement, because there is no hope of reaching them through any means of experiment and observation. And the path to certainty about them is only the news of God, Exalted is His Majesty, or the mutawatir Sunnah. This is with regard to sensible matters. As for claims related to an abstract or unseen matter not subject to any of the external senses, among them are those for which you find a clear text in the Book or mutawatir Sunnah, and among them are those for which you do not find a clear statement in either of them. As for what is explicitly stated in either of them, it enters by that into certain perceptions. And the way of certainty about it is that from the perspective of the transmission of the Book or Sunnah of it, it goes back to the certain mutawatir report which we have finished researching. For the Qur'an is only the word revealed to the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) and reaching us by way of tawatur, so there is no doubt that the Qur'ān-ness of its words is definitively established. And like the Qur'an in that is the Sunnah if it reaches us as mutawatir.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument regarding unseen matters (الإخبارات الغيبية). The author contrasts the Qur'an's treatment of empirical matters (which are left to reason) with unseen matters: these are detailed decisively because experiment and observation cannot access them. Certainty about them comes only from God's news or the mutawatir Sunnah. He then divides claims that are abstract or unseen into two categories: those that have an explicit text in the Qur'an or mutawatir Sunnah, and those that do not. The first category enters into certain perceptions (المدركات اليقينية). The reason for certainty is that the transmission of the text is by tawatur (mass transmission), which he has already established as yielding certainty. The Qur'anic text in particular is definitively transmitted from the Prophet via tawatur, so its words are certain. The same applies to mutawatir Sunnah. Thus, any explicit text in these sources provides certainty about its content without needing rational proof.

Key Terms
الإخبارات الغيبية
التواتر
المدركات اليقينية
خبر الله
السنة المتواترة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all unseen matters require rational proof or empirical evidence to be certain.
Correction: The passage clarifies that certainty about unseen matters comes exclusively from revelation transmitted via tawatur, not from human reason or observation.

Checkpoint
How does a person achieve certainty about unseen matters according to this passage?
A) Through scientific experiment and observation
B) Through rational deduction alone
C) Only through the news of God or mutawatir Sunnah
D) Through consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Certainty about unseen matters is derived exclusively from revelation transmitted via tawatur, not from empirical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the unseen news from the Qur'an—like the description of Paradise, Hell, and the Day of Judgment—with the same unwavering certainty you have for things you see with your own eyes? When doubts creep in, what do you do?
?
The lesson says certainty about the unseen comes only through tawatur, not personal reasoning. Are there unseen matters you've accepted based on weak evidence or cultural stories rather than the Qur'an and mutawatir Sunnah? How will you correct that?
?
If someone asked you tomorrow why you are absolutely sure that the unseen matters in Islam are true, would your answer reflect the tawatur-based certainty described here—or would it be vague? Write down your answer now.

Unseen Matters
beginner

Reason's Role in Unseen Matters: Revelation and Rational Inquiry
Classification of unseen matters by accessibility to reason and role of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between unseen matters that are only known through revelation and those that can be investigated by reason, and how the Qur'an encourages rational reflection on the latter.

Core Idea
Certain unseen matters are beyond reason; others invite rational reflection.

This passage from Buti clarifies the scope of certainty concerning the unseen. It distinguishes between matters known solely through revelation, like the details of the afterlife, and those that reason can explore, such as God's existence and causality. The author also highlights that the Qur'an itself encourages such rational investigation, and that scholars of kalam have taken up this task.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation slowly. Pay attention to the examples given.
Notice the phrases 'سيان بعد ذلك' and 'غير أن من شأن القرآن'.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two types of unseen matters: those reason cannot reach (sam'iyyat) and those reason can investigate. List the examples for each.
Examples for first: the Hour, resurrection, Paradise, Hell, angels. Examples for second: God's existence, origination of contingent things, causality.
3
Understand the epistemological claim
The author states that even matters reason cannot grasp become certain perceptions if they are attested by a clear text from Qur'an or mutawatir Sunnah. This shows the sufficiency of revelation for certainty.
4
Reflect on the Qur'an's role
The passage notes that the Qur'an impels us to reflect and consider the unseen matters that are accessible to reason. This is a key point about the compatibility of faith and reason.
This leads to the engagement of kalam scholars.
5
Consider the implication for kalam
The passage concludes that scholars of kalam have indeed discussed these issues using reason and thought, indicating that rational theology is legitimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسيان بعد ذلك ، أن يكون للعقل سبيل إلى هضم هذه المغيبات وفهمها عن طريقه الخاص ، أو أن لا يكون له إليها من سبيل ، كتلك المغيبات السمعية التي لم ينفذ إلينا شيء من أمرها إلا عن طريق الخبر الصادق عنها كقيام الساعة وحشر ووجود الجنة والنار والملائكة ، فكل ذلك يكفي لدخوله في المدركات اليقينية أن نصاً صريحاً من كتاب الله أو متواتر السنة قد تناوله وأخبر عنه • غير أن من شأن القرآن مع ذلك أن يحملنا على التأمل والنظر في كل ما يخبرنا عنه ويحملنا على اليقين به ، من تلك الغيبيات التي يمكن للعقل البشري أن يجول ويلمس الحقيقة عنها ، كوجود الله عز وجل وحدوث الممكنات وجعلية الأسباب الكونية وماشابه ذلك ، ولقد خاض علماء الكلام في بحث هذه المسائل ، عن طريق العقل والفكر - ٣٩ -
And it is equal after that whether the intellect has a way to digest these unseen things and understand them by its own method, or it has no way to them, like those auditory unseen matters of which nothing has reached us except through the truthful report about them, such as the establishment of the Hour, the resurrection, the existence of Paradise and Hell, and the angels. For all of that, it suffices for its entry into certain perceptions that an explicit text from the Book of God or the mutawatir Sunnah has dealt with it and informed about it. However, it is a characteristic of the Qur'an, despite that, to impel us to reflection and consideration in everything it informs us about and impels us to certainty of, among those unseen matters which the human intellect can roam and touch the reality of, such as the existence of God Almighty, the origination of contingent things, the causality of cosmic causes, and the like. And the scholars of kalam have engaged in the discussion of these issues, by way of reason and thought.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scope of certainty and the role of reason regarding unseen matters. The author states that it is equal (سواء) whether reason can comprehend the unseen matter or not—in either case, if an explicit text from the Qur'an or mutawatir Sunnah attests to it, it enters into certain perceptions. He gives examples of matters reason cannot grasp: the Hour, resurrection, Paradise, Hell, angels—these are known only through truthful report (الخبر الصادق). However, the author then notes a complementary characteristic of the Qur'an: it also impels us to reflect on and reason about those unseen matters that the human intellect can investigate, such as God's existence, the origination of contingent things, and the causality of cosmic causes. He concludes that the scholars of kalam have indeed engaged in discussing these issues through reason and thought. Thus, the passage balances between purely transmitted knowledge and rational investigation, showing that both have a place in the Islamic epistemological framework.

Key Terms
المغيبات السمعية
الخبر الصادق
المدركات اليقينية
التأمل والنظر
حدوث الممكنات
جعلية الأسباب الكونية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think all unseen matters are beyond the reach of reason and can only be believed by blind faith.
Correction: This passage clarifies that while some unseen matters are beyond reason, others are accessible to rational investigation, and the Qur'an itself encourages such reflection.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is required for an unseen matter to be considered certain knowledge?
A) That human reason can understand it
B) That it is attested by an explicit text from the Qur'an or mutawatir Sunnah
C) That it is a matter of sensory experience
D) That it is agreed upon by all philosophers

Key Takeaway
The unseen includes both matters beyond reason that are known through revelation and matters accessible to rational inquiry, and the Qur'an encourages reflection on the latter, which scholars of kalam have undertaken.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'anic accounts of Paradise and Hell, do you submit to them as certain truths through the text alone, or do you secretly wish for rational proof? The passage says the text suffices for certainty. Where is your heart in that submission?
?
You believe in God, but have you ever truly pondered the evidence for His existence using your own reason, as the Qur'an impels you to? Or have you relied solely on what you were taught? What would it take for you to engage your intellect deeply in this reflection?
?
The scholars of kalam used reason to discuss matters like God's existence and causality. Do you shy away from such intellectual engagement, perhaps fearing doubt? How can you commit to using the mind God gave you to strengthen your certainty, starting today with a specific question about His attributes?

Unseen Matters

Certainty from Transmitted Reports
Buti affirms that revealed unseen matters yield certainty regardless of rational grasp.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that transmitted unseen matters reported by an explicit text from the Quran or mutawatir Sunnah are considered certain perceptions, even if reason cannot grasp them.

Core Idea
Certainty about the unseen comes from revelation, not rational proof.

Buti addresses a key question: how can we be certain about matters beyond our sensory and rational experience, like the afterlife? He argues that the truthfulness of the report (Quran or mutawatir Sunnah) suffices for certainty, regardless of whether reason can comprehend the reported content. This lesson unpacks that claim and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm and key terms. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice the phrase 'سواء...أو لا يكون له إليها من سبيل' – it sets up the contrast.
2
Identify the key claim
Buti states that it makes no difference whether reason can digest these unseen matters or not. The deciding factor is the source: a truthful report.
The 'truthful report' here is defined as an explicit text from Quran or mutawatir Sunnah.
3
Examine the examples
Buti lists four examples of transmitted unseen matters: the Day of Resurrection, gathering of bodies, Paradise and Hell, and angels. These are known only through revelation.
These are classic sam'iyyat – matters known through hearing (scripture) rather than reason.
4
Understand the condition for certainty
The passage concludes that for such matters, an explicit text from Quran or mutawatir Sunnah is sufficient to include them in 'certain perceptions' (al-mudrakat al-yaqiniyyah).
This establishes a methodological principle: revelation provides certainty even when reason is silent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسيان بعد ذلك أن يكون للعقل سبيل إلى هضم هذه المغيبات وفهمها عن طريقه الخاص أو أن لا يكون له إليها من سبيل كتلك المغيبات السمعية التي لم ينفذ إلينا شيء من أمرها إلا عن طريق الخبر الصادق عنها كقيام الساعة وحشر الأجساد ووجود الجنة والنار والملائكة فكل ذلك يكفي لدخوله في المدركات اليقينية أن نصاً صريحاً من كتاب الله أو متواتر السنة قد تناوله وأخبر عنه.
And it is the same after that whether the intellect has a way to digest these unseen things and understand them by its own method or it has no way to them, like those transmitted unseen matters concerning which nothing of their affair reached us except through truthful report about them, such as the Day of Resurrection, the gathering of bodies, the existence of Paradise and Hell, and the angels. All of that is sufficient for it to enter into the certain perceptions that an explicit text from the Book of Allah or the mutawatir Sunnah has addressed it and informed about it.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage establishes that certainty about unseen matters does not depend on rational accessibility. Buti states that it is equal whether the intellect can comprehend these unseen things by its own method or not. He gives examples of 'transmitted unseen matters' (al-mughibat al-sam'iyyah): the Day of Resurrection, the gathering of bodies, the existence of Paradise and Hell, and the angels. These are known only through truthful reports. The condition for certainty is that an explicit text from the Quran or the mutawatir Sunnah addresses and informs about them. Thus, for such matters, a clear scriptural source suffices to include them in the category of certain perceptions (al-mudrakat al-yaqiniyyah). The passage clarifies that the epistemic value of revelation is not contingent on rational verification.

Key Terms
الخبر الصادق
المغيبات السمعية
المتواتر
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matters like the afterlife are only probable because they cannot be proven by reason.
Correction: Buti affirms they are certain if reported by a clear text from Quran or mutawatir Sunnah, regardless of rational accessibility.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, what is sufficient for a transmitted unseen matter to be considered certain?
A) Rational proof
B) A clear text from Quran or mutawatir Sunnah
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Personal spiritual experience

Key Takeaway
Revelation from Quran and authentic Sunnah provides certainty about the unseen, independent of rational verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any unseen matter reported in the Quran that you find difficult to accept because it seems irrational? How does this passage challenge you to submit your intellect to the authority of revelation?
?
You may have relied on your own reasoning to confirm certain Islamic beliefs. But according to this lesson, what is the true basis for certainty about the unseen? Are you willing to accept matters on the basis of report alone, even if your mind cannot fully grasp them?
?
Think of a specific unseen reality (like angels or the punishment of the grave). Have you ever doubted it because you could not conceive of it? What would it mean for your faith if you were to treat the Quranic text as sufficient for certainty, as Buti argues?

The Quranic Arrangement Is Divinely Ordained
Buti refutes that Uthman arranged the Quran, citing tawqeef and two narrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the arrangement of the Quran's surahs and verses is based on divine instruction (tawqeef), not human decision.

Core Idea
The Quran's order is fixed by divine decree, not by any human.

Buti addresses a common misconception that Caliph Uthman personally arranged the Quran according to his own preference. He uses a rhetorical question to challenge the basis of this claim, then presents the correct Islamic belief: the arrangement is based on tawqeef, divine instruction, with two key narrations as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand Buti's argument.
Notice the rhetorical question style.
2
Understand the rhetorical question
Identify what claim Buti is refuting. He asks: from which source is it established that Uthman divided and arranged the Quran by his own desire?
3
Grasp the concept of tawqeef
Buti asserts that the arrangement of verses and surahs is based on tawqeef (divine instruction), and even the Prophet had no hand in it.
Tawqeef means the order is divinely determined, not humanly devised.
4
Examine the first evidence
Read the hadith from al-Bukhari: Uthman says 'I do not change anything from its place' regarding an abrogated verse. This shows the order is fixed.
This indicates Uthman considered the arrangement immutable.
5
Examine the second evidence
Read the narration from al-Qurtubi: Rabi'a explains why al-Baqarah and Al 'Imran are placed first despite later revelation. He says the Quran was compiled on the knowledge of the one who compiled it, implying divine guidance.
This reinforces that the order is not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن أي مصدر استقرائي أو استدلالي أو استنتاجي ثبت أن عثمان هو الذي قسم القرآن إلى سور وآيات ، وأنه عمد فرتبها كما شاء له هواه ، وأن هواه قـد شـاء له أن ترتب بدءاً بأطولها ، علماً بأنه هو الذي فصل هذه طويلة وتلك قصيرة ؟ ..
أما نحن ، فالذي نعلمه طبقاً للرواية الصحيحة الثابتة من رسول الله الله ومن عثمان نفسه، أن أمر الآيات وترتيبها والسور وتقسيمها وترتيبها مرد كل ذلك إلى التوقيف الذي لم يكن حتى لرسول الله الله يـد فيـه ودليلنا على ذلك ما رواه البخاري بسنده عن ابن الزبير قال : قلت لعثمان : هذه الآية في [ البقرة : ٢٤٠ ] ﴿ والدِّينَ يُتَوَفوَّنَ مِنْكُمْ ويَذرون أزواجاً ) إلى قوله : ﴿ غَيرَ إخراج قد نسختها الآية الأخرى ، فلم تكتبها ؟ قال : يا ابن أخي : أنا لا أغير شيئاً من مكانه . وما رواه القرطبي وغيره بسند صحيح عن سليمان بن بلال قال سمعت ربيعة يسأل : لم قدمت البقرة وآل عمران وقد نزل قبلها بضع وثمانون سورة وإنما نزلتا في المدينة ؟ فقال ربيعة : قد قدمتا ، وإنما ألف القرآن على علم ممن ألفه
From which inductive, inferential, or deductive source is it established that Uthman is the one who divided the Quran into surahs and verses, and that he deliberately arranged them as his whim desired, and that his whim desired that they be arranged beginning with the longest, knowing that he is the one who distinguished some as long and others as short?.. As for us, what we know according to the authentic narration from the Messenger of Allah and from Uthman himself is that the matter of the verses and their arrangement, and the surahs and their division and arrangement, all of that is referred back to divine instruction (tawqeef) in which even the Messenger of Allah had no hand. Our evidence for that is what al-Bukhari narrated with his chain from Ibn al-Zubayr: he said, I said to Uthman: This verse in [al-Baqarah: 240] 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave wives' up to His saying 'without expulsion' has been abrogated by the other verse, so why do you write it? He said: O son of my brother, I do not change anything from its place. And what al-Qurtubi and others narrated with a sound chain from Sulayman ibn Bilal: he said, I heard Rabi'a being asked: Why were al-Baqarah and Al 'Imran placed first when eighty-some surahs were revealed before them, and they were only revealed in Medina? So Rabi'a said: They were placed first, and the Quran was compiled on the knowledge of the one who compiled it.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Caliph Uthman personally arranged the Quran's surahs and verses according to his own preference. Buti first poses a rhetorical question challenging the source of such an assertion. He then states the correct belief: the arrangement of the Quran is based on tawqeef (divine decree), and even the Prophet himself had no independent role in it. He provides two pieces of evidence: first, a hadith from al-Bukhari where Uthman, when questioned about including a verse that was abrogated, replies, 'I do not change anything from its place.' This shows Uthman considered the arrangement fixed. Second, a narration from al-Qurtubi where Rabi'a explains that al-Baqarah and Al 'Imran were placed first despite being revealed later, and states that the Quran was compiled based on the knowledge of the one who compiled it—implying divine instruction. Thus, the order is not human invention.

Key Terms
التوقيف
ترتيب السور
ترتيب الآيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Caliph Uthman personally arranged the surahs and verses of the Quran according to his own preference and whim.
Correction: The correct belief is that the arrangement of the Quran is based on divine instruction (tawqeef), and even the Prophet had no hand in it, as evidenced by Uthman's statement and Rabi'a's narration.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Buti provide from al-Bukhari to prove that the arrangement of the Quran is divinely ordained?
A) Uthman said he arranged the surahs by length.
B) Uthman said 'I do not change anything from its place' when asked about an abrogated verse.
C) The Prophet said the Quran was arranged by Jibril.
D) Rabi'a said the Quran was compiled by the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The arrangement of the Quran's surahs and verses is not a human invention but is based on divine instruction (tawqeef), confirmed by authentic narrations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat every verse of the Quran as placed exactly where Allah intended it, or do you sometimes skip around based on your own convenience? How might this lesson change your approach to reading the Quran?
?
Uthman said, 'I do not change anything from its place.' Are there areas in your life where you are tempted to change or rearrange what Allah has ordained? What would it mean for you to say, 'I do not change anything from its place'?
?
This passage shows that even the Prophet had no hand in the arrangement. Do you fully trust that the Quran as it is—every surah, every verse—is exactly what you need? What would change if you lived with that conviction?

Unseen Matters
intermediate
6 min
The Second Argument: What is Conditioned on the Possible is Possible
Explains the argument from the verse of Moses about 'lan' and the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter, based on the verse of Moses and the linguistic analysis of 'lan'.

Core Idea
If Allah conditioned His vision upon a possible event, then vision itself must be possible.

This passage presents a key argument from classical Sunni theology for the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter. It builds on the famous verse where Allah tells Moses 'You will not see Me' (lan tarāni), but then conditions seeing upon the stability of the mountain. The author argues that this very conditioning proves vision is possible. He also refutes a linguistic objection that 'lan' implies eternal negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If the mountain remains, then you will see Me.'
2
Identify the core argument
The author presents a logical conditional: if X is conditioned on Y, and Y is possible, then X must also be possible. Here, X is seeing Allah, Y is the mountain's stability. Since Y is possible, X is possible.
This is the main proof; everything else is support or refutation.
3
Understand the refutation of al-Zamakhshari
Al-Zamakhshari claimed 'lan' means eternal negation. The author refutes by showing 'lan' is for emphasis, not eternity, and it is often qualified by 'abadan'. Even if it were eternal, it would only apply to this world.
The Quranic verse 2:95 is used as an example: 'lan' is used but the wish for death occurs in the afterlife.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
This argument is one of several proofs for the possibility (jawāz) of seeing Allah in the Hereafter, which is a doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah. It counters those who deny the vision altogether.
Remember: possibility does not mean it will happen; it means it is not rationally impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الله تعالى علق رؤيته له على شيء جائز وهو استقرار الجبل إذ هو أمر ممكن في ذاته ضرورة كما هو معلوم ، وما عُلق على الممكن لا بد أن يكون هو أيضاً ممكناً . وليست لن للتأييد كما زعم الزمخشري ، بل هي للتأكيد ، ولذلك تُقيَّد بأبداً . وإن سلم أنها للتأييد فإنه يكون في الدنيا ، كقوله تعالى ﴿ وَلَنْ يَتَمَنَّوْهُ أَبَداً بما قَدَّمَتْ أَيْدِيهِمْ ﴾ [البقرة : ٩٥] . مع أنهم يتمنون الموت في الآخرة للخلاص من العذاب
The second: That Allah Most High conditioned His vision upon something possible, namely the mountain's stability, since it is a possible matter in itself necessarily, as is known. And what is conditioned upon the possible must also be possible. And 'lan' is not for eternity as al-Zamakhshari claimed, rather it is for emphasis, and therefore it is qualified by 'abadan'. And if it is conceded that it is for eternity, then it would be in this world, like His saying Most High: 'And they will never wish for it ever, because of what their hands have sent forth' [al-Baqarah: 95], while they will wish for death in the Hereafter to be saved from punishment.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter. It is a proof based on the verse of Moses (peace be upon him) where Allah said, 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me' (Quran 7:143). The author argues that Allah conditioned His vision upon something possible—the stability of the mountain—because the mountain's stability is intrinsically possible. Whatever is conditioned upon a possible thing must itself be possible. Therefore, seeing Allah is possible. The author then addresses al-Zamakhshari's claim that the word 'lan' (will not) in the Arabic language indicates eternal negation. The author refutes this by stating that 'lan' is for emphasis, not eternity, and it is often qualified by 'abadan' (ever). Even if it were for eternity, the verse would only negate vision in this world, not in the Hereafter. The author supports this with Quran 2:95, where 'lan' is used for the disbelievers' wish for death, yet they will wish for death in the Hereafter. Thus, the argument establishes the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Key Terms

لن
التأكيد
التأييد
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Lan' indicates eternal negation in the Quran.
Correction: 'Lan' primarily indicates emphasis and does not necessarily imply eternity; it can be restricted to a specific context (e.g., this world) as shown by the author's analysis and Quran 2:95.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the word 'lan' (لن) in the verse 'lan tarāni' indicate?
A) Eternal negation
B) Emphasis and not eternity
C) Possibility
D) Impossibility

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing Allah is supported by the conditional in the Quran and the linguistic analysis that 'lan' does not imply eternal negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live with the hope of seeing Allah, or has that possibility become a distant doctrine? What one change in your daily worship would you make if you were certain you will see Him?
?
The author argued that 'lan' does not mean eternal negation. Are there any verses of promise or threat you have dismissed as 'not for me'? How does this lesson challenge you to take Allah's words more personally?
?
If seeing Allah is possible, what does that possibility demand from you today? What are you actively doing to prepare for that encounter, and what are you neglecting?

Consensus on Seeing Allah and the Shafi'i Anecdote
States the consensus of Muslims (except Mu'tazila) on vision of Allah and recounts al-Shafi'i's decisive statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the belief in seeing Allah in the Hereafter is a consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah, and how Imam al-Shafi'i considered it fundamental to worship.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is the ultimate goal of Paradise, and al-Shafi'i said certainty of it is essential for worship.

The author presents the unanimous agreement of Muslims, except the Mu'tazila, on seeing Allah in the Hereafter. He then recounts a powerful story of Imam al-Shafi'i, who tied his very worship to this belief. This lesson explores both the consensus and the anecdote that underscores its centrality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus and its exception
Read the first part of the passage where the author states the unanimity of Muslims except the Mu'tazila. Notice that this consensus is based on the clarity of proofs. Consider why the Mu'tazila are excepted.
The Mu'tazila denied the vision of Allah because they thought it implied corporeality.
2
Reflect on the Shafi'i anecdote
Read the story of al-Rabi' and al-Shafi'i. Pay attention to al-Shafi'i's deduction from the Quranic verse and his strong oath. Ask yourself why he considered this belief so important.
3
Grasp the significance of the belief
Think about how the hope of seeing Allah motivated the righteous and how al-Shafi'i linked it to the very act of worship. This shows the belief is not just theoretical but foundational to faith and practice.
This step connects the two parts of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوضوح هذه الأدلة أطبق عامة المسلمين ما عدا المعتزلة على أن الصالحين من العباد يرون الله عز وجل يوم القيامة ، ومن أجل ذلك تعلقت آمال كثير من المقربين من نعيم الجنة كلها برؤية الله عزّ وجل ، وعاشوا في الدنيا وهم لا يمنون أنفسهم من نعيم الآخرة إلا بها . وقد ذكر الربيع رحمه الله أنه كان ذات يوم عند الشافعي ، وجاءه كتاب من الصعيد يسأل فيه كاتبه عن قوله عز وجل : ﴿ كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَنْ رَبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لمحجوبونَ ﴾ [ المطففين ١٥] فكتب : لما حجب قوماً بالسخط دل على أن قوماً يرونه بالرضى . فقال له الربيع : أو تدين بهذا يا سيدي ؟ فقال : والله لو لم يوقن محمد بن إدريس أنه يرى ربه في المعاد لما عبده في الدنيا
And due to the clarity of these proofs, the general body of Muslims, except the Mu'tazila, unanimously agreed that the righteous servants will see Allah Almighty on the Day of Judgment. For that reason, the hopes of many of the near ones (to Allah) were attached to the vision of Allah Almighty above all the bliss of Paradise, and they lived in this world while never promising themselves any bliss of the Hereafter except it. And al-Rabi' (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned that one day he was with al-Shafi'i, and a letter came to him from al-Sa'id (Upper Egypt) in which its writer asked about His saying Almighty: 'No! Indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled' (al-Mutaffifin 15). So he wrote: 'When He veiled a people with displeasure, it indicates that a people see Him with pleasure.' Al-Rabi' said to him: 'Do you profess this, O my master?' He replied: 'By Allah, if Muhammad ibn Idris were not certain that he will see his Lord in the afterlife, he would not have worshipped Him in this world.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a declaration of the unanimity of Ahl al-Sunnah (except the Mu'tazila) regarding the vision of Allah in the Hereafter. The author emphasizes that the proofs for this are so clear that the general Muslim consensus has formed. He then notes that the hopes of the spiritually elite are centered on this vision above all other pleasures of Paradise. To illustrate the importance of this belief, he relates an anecdote involving Imam al-Shafi'i and his companion al-Rabi'. In the story, al-Shafi'i receives a letter asking about the verse 'Indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled' (al-Mutaffifin 15). Al-Shafi'i deduces that if the disbelievers are veiled with displeasure, then the believers must see Allah with pleasure. When al-Rabi' asks if he truly holds this view, al-Shafi'i swears by Allah that if he were not certain he would see his Lord in the Hereafter, he would not have worshipped Him in this world. This underscores the centrality of the belief in ru'ya to the faith of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Key Terms
رؤية الله
إجماع
الصالحين
المقربين
الشافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the vision of Allah is a minor or optional issue in Islam.
Correction: This passage shows that it is so fundamental that al-Shafi'i considered certainty of it essential to his worship, and it is a consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Checkpoint
What did Imam al-Shafi'i say about the belief in seeing Allah in the Hereafter?
A) It is optional
B) It is a matter of interpretation
C) He would not have worshipped Allah without it
D) It is only for prophets

Key Takeaway
The belief in seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment is a core tenet of Ahl al-Sunnah, so central that al-Shafi'i declared it essential to his worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Shafi'i said that if he were not certain he would see his Lord in the afterlife, he would not have worshipped Him. How certain are you about the realities of the unseen that you claim to believe? Would your worship collapse if that certainty were shaken?
?
Your hopes in this life—are they attached to the vision of Allah as the greatest bliss, or are they consumed by worldly pleasures? What would it take for you to truly live with the same longing as the muqarrabun?
?
If someone asked you, 'Do you really profess that you will see Allah with your eyes on the Day of Judgment?' could you answer with the same conviction as al-Shafi'i, or would you hesitate? What needs to change in your heart for you to be able to say 'By Allah, I am certain'?

The First Opinion: Denial of Seeing Allah in This World
Presents the first opinion led by Aisha with evidence from hadith and Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Sunni opinion that revelation only indicates vision of Allah in the Hereafter, and the evidence from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her).

Core Idea
Aisha's stance: No human sees Allah in this world.

This lesson examines the first of two Sunni opinions on whether Allah can be seen in this world. Led by Aisha, this view holds that revelation only speaks of vision in the Hereafter and forbids it before death. We will explore the key hadith and Quranic verses she used to defend this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first opinion
Focus on the part describing the view that revelation only indicates vision in the Hereafter, and points to the hadith from Ubadah ibn al-Samit.
Notice that Aisha is called the leader of this opinion.
2
Examine Aisha's three statements
Aisha refuted three claims: seeing the Lord, knowing the unseen, and concealing revelation. Identify the verses she recited for each.
She used strong language: 'whoever tells you... has lied'.
3
Understand the exception
Aisha affirmed that the Prophet saw Jibril in his true form twice, not Allah.
This clarifies that vision of angels is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجانب الثالث : وهو البحث في أنه هل دل السمع على وقوع الرؤية أو إمكان وقوعها لأحد من الناس في الدنيا ؟ فقد اختلف في ذلك أهل السنة والجماعة إلى مذهبين : فمنهم من قال لم يرد السمع إلا بما يدل على الرؤية في الآخرة فقط ، بل الذي جاء به السمع هو امتناع رؤية أحد من الناس ربه قبل الموت وذلك للحديث المرفوع الذي رواه مسلم عن عبادة بن الصامت أنه قال : « واعلموا أنكم لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا » وزعيم القائلين بهذا الرأي من الصحابة السيدة عائشة رضي الله عنها ، فقد روى البخاري وغيره عن مسروق قال : قلت لعائشة رضي عنها : يا أماه هل رأى محمد ربه ؟ فقالت : لقد قف شعري مما قلت ، أين أنت من ثلاث من حدثكهن فقد كذب : من حدثك أن محمداً رأى ربه فقد كذب ، ثم قرأت : ( لا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ وَهُوَ اللطيف الخَبِيرُ ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٠٣ ] وما كانَ لِبَشَرِ أَنْ يُكَلِّمَهُ اللهُ إِلا وَحْيا أَوْ مِنْ وَراء حجاب ﴾ [الشورى : ٥١] . ومن حدثك أنه يعلم ما في غد فقد كذب . ثم قرأت : ﴿ وَمَا تُدْري نَفْسٌ ماذا تَكْسِبُ غِداً ﴾ [لقمان : ٣٤ ] . ومن حدثك أنــه كتم فقد كذب وقرأت قوله تعالى : ( يا أيها الرسولُ بَلِّغ ما أُنْزِلَ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [ المائدة : ٦٧] . ولكن رأى جبريل عليه السلام في صورته مرتين . وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنه قد دل السمع على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في دار الدنيا وزعيم القائلين بهذا هو عبـد الله الله بن عباس رضي عنه ومعه جمهور الصحابة . ومن أهم أدلتهم على ذلك حديث الإسراء والمعراج وقول الله تعالى : وَما جَعَلْنَا الرُّؤْيا التي أرَيْناكَ إِلا فِتْنَة للناس ) [ الإسراء : ٦٠ ] ، وأحاديث كثيرة وردت في ذلك . وقد أنكر الفريق الأول صحة الاستدلال بهذه الآية : مستدلين بأن ( الرؤيا ) بالألف تطلق على الرؤيا المنامية لا الحقيقة . إلا أن الجواب على ذلك هو أن الرؤيا كما تطلق على رؤيا النوم فإنها تطلق أيضاً على رؤيا اليقظة دون تفريق . وذلك لقول الشاعر : فكبر للرؤيا وهش فـــــؤاده وبشر قلباً كان جماً بلابله
As for the third aspect: it is the inquiry into whether revelation indicates the occurrence or possibility of vision for any person in this world. Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah differed on this into two schools. Among them are those who said that revelation only indicates vision in the Hereafter, but rather what revelation has brought is the impossibility of any person seeing his Lord before death, due to the raised hadith which Muslim narrated from 'Ubadah ibn al-Samit that he said: 'Know that you will not see your Lord until you die.' The leader of this opinion among the Companions is Lady 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her. Al-Bukhari and others narrated from Masruq that he said: I said to 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'O mother, did Muhammad see his Lord?' She said: 'My hair stood on end from what you said. Where are you from three things: whoever tells you that Muhammad saw his Lord has lied.' Then she recited: 'Vision does not perceive Him, but He perceives all vision, and He is the Subtle, the Aware' (al-An'am 103), and 'It is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil' (al-Shura 51). And whoever tells you that he knows what will be tomorrow has lied. Then she recited: 'No soul knows what it will earn tomorrow' (Luqman 34). And whoever tells you that he concealed has lied, and she recited: 'O Messenger, convey that which has been sent down to you' (al-Ma'idah 67). But he saw Jibril, peace be upon him, in his form twice. And the majority went that revelation has indicated the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in the abode of this world, and the leader of this opinion is 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, along with the majority of the Companions. Among their most important proofs on that is the hadith of al-Isra' and al-Mi'raj and the saying of Allah Most High: 'And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind' (al-Isra' 60), and many hadiths have been narrated in that regard. The first group denied the validity of using this verse as evidence, arguing that 'al-ru'ya' (with alif) is used for the dream vision, not reality. However, the response to that is that 'al-ru'ya', just as it is used for the dream vision, is also used for the waking vision without distinction. This is supported by the saying of the poet: 'He said 'Allahu Akbar' for the vision and his heart cheered, and gladdened a heart that had been full of calamities.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage discusses the third aspect of the vision of Allah: whether revelation indicates that it can occur or be possible in this world. The author states that Ahl al-Sunnah split into two opinions. The first opinion, led by 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her), holds that revelation only indicates vision in the Hereafter and actually negates it before death. This is based on the hadith 'You will not see your Lord until you die' (narrated by Muslim) and 'A'isha's own statements. 'A'isha vehemently denied that the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw his Lord in this life, citing verses that vision does not encompass Allah (al-An'am 103) and that Allah only speaks to humans through revelation or from behind a veil (al-Shura 51). She also denied any knowledge of the unseen or concealment of revelation. She affirmed that the Prophet only saw Jibril in his true form twice. The second opinion, led by 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him), is that revelation permits seeing Allah in this world. Their main evidence includes the verse in surah al-Isra' (17:60) about the vision shown to the Prophet during the Night Journey, along with many hadiths. The first group objected to this reasoning, arguing that 'al-ru'ya' (with a definite article and alif) refers to a dream vision, not a waking vision. The author responds by explaining that 'al-ru'ya' is used for both dream and waking visions without distinction, citing a line of poetry where the poet refers to a waking vision. Thus, the objection is refuted. The passage does not definitively settle the issue but presents the two views and the counter-argument.

Key Terms
رؤية الله في الدنيا
عائشة
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet saw Allah in this world on the Night of Isra'.
Correction: Aisha refutes this strongly; the vision was of Jibril, not Allah.

Checkpoint
Which Companion is the leader of the opinion that seeing Allah in this world is impossible before death?
A) Ibn Abbas
B) Ubadah ibn al-Samit
C) Aisha
D) Masruq

Key Takeaway
Aisha's position, supported by hadith and Quran, affirms that no human can see Allah in this world; only Jibril was seen in his true form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Aisha's hair stood on end when asked if Muhammad saw his Lord. When you speak about Allah—whether in dua, conversation, or teaching—does your heart tremble with the same reverence and awe, or have you grown careless with the words you use about your Creator? What can you do today to restore that sacred fear?
?
You know that no soul knows what it will earn tomorrow—yet how often do you make plans as if the future is in your hands? Where are you holding onto a false certainty that should be surrendered to Allah? Will you practice saying 'insha'Allah' with full awareness today, acknowledging that only He knows what tomorrow holds?
?
The Prophet conveyed everything revealed to him without concealment. You too have been entrusted with a message—whether knowledge, a good deed, or a truth that needs to be spoken. What part of that message are you hiding because you fear people's reactions or discomfort? Who is your fear really serving, and are you ready to emulate the Messenger's complete honesty?

The Second Opinion: Possibility of Seeing Allah in This World
Presents the second opinion led by Ibn Abbas with evidence, objection, and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Sunni opinion that revelation permits seeing Allah in this world, the evidence from Ibn Abbas, and the refutation of an objection based on the word 'al-ru'ya'.

Core Idea
Ibn Abbas and majority: Seeing Allah in this world is possible.

This lesson covers the second opinion led by Ibn Abbas, who holds that revelation indicates the possibility of seeing Allah in this world. We will examine their key proof from the Isra' verse, the objection raised by the first group, and the response that 'al-ru'ya' can mean both dream and waking vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second opinion
Focus on the part describing the view of the majority led by Ibn Abbas, including their evidence from the Isra' verse and hadiths.
Notice the objection from the first group follows immediately.
2
Analyze the objection
The first group objected that 'al-ru'ya' in the verse means dream vision, not reality. Understand their reasoning.
They used the spelling 'al-ru'ya' with alif.
3
Study the response
The author responds that 'al-ru'ya' is used for both dream and waking vision without distinction, citing a line of poetry as evidence.
The poetic evidence supports the broad usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجانب الثالث : وهو البحث في أنه هل دل السمع على وقوع الرؤية أو إمكان وقوعها لأحد من الناس في الدنيا ؟ فقد اختلف في ذلك أهل السنة والجماعة إلى مذهبين : فمنهم من قال لم يرد السمع إلا بما يدل على الرؤية في الآخرة فقط ، بل الذي جاء به السمع هو امتناع رؤية أحد من الناس ربه قبل الموت وذلك للحديث المرفوع الذي رواه مسلم عن عبادة بن الصامت أنه قال : « واعلموا أنكم لن تروا ربكم حتى تموتوا » وزعيم القائلين بهذا الرأي من الصحابة السيدة عائشة رضي الله عنها ، فقد روى البخاري وغيره عن مسروق قال : قلت لعائشة رضي عنها : يا أماه هل رأى محمد ربه ؟ فقالت : لقد قف شعري مما قلت ، أين أنت من ثلاث من حدثكهن فقد كذب : من حدثك أن محمداً رأى ربه فقد كذب ، ثم قرأت : ( لا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ وَهُوَ اللطيف الخَبِيرُ ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٠٣ ] وما كانَ لِبَشَرِ أَنْ يُكَلِّمَهُ اللهُ إِلا وَحْيا أَوْ مِنْ وَراء حجاب ﴾ [الشورى : ٥١] . ومن حدثك أنه يعلم ما في غد فقد كذب . ثم قرأت : ﴿ وَمَا تُدْري نَفْسٌ ماذا تَكْسِبُ غِداً ﴾ [لقمان : ٣٤ ] . ومن حدثك أنــه كتم فقد كذب وقرأت قوله تعالى : ( يا أيها الرسولُ بَلِّغ ما أُنْزِلَ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [ المائدة : ٦٧] . ولكن رأى جبريل عليه السلام في صورته مرتين . وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنه قد دل السمع على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في دار الدنيا وزعيم القائلين بهذا هو عبـد الله الله بن عباس رضي عنه ومعه جمهور الصحابة . ومن أهم أدلتهم على ذلك حديث الإسراء والمعراج وقول الله تعالى : وَما جَعَلْنَا الرُّؤْيا التي أرَيْناكَ إِلا فِتْنَة للناس ) [ الإسراء : ٦٠ ] ، وأحاديث كثيرة وردت في ذلك . وقد أنكر الفريق الأول صحة الاستدلال بهذه الآية : مستدلين بأن ( الرؤيا ) بالألف تطلق على الرؤيا المنامية لا الحقيقة . إلا أن الجواب على ذلك هو أن الرؤيا كما تطلق على رؤيا النوم فإنها تطلق أيضاً على رؤيا اليقظة دون تفريق . وذلك لقول الشاعر : فكبر للرؤيا وهش فـــــؤاده وبشر قلباً كان جماً بلابله
As for the third aspect: it is the inquiry into whether revelation indicates the occurrence or possibility of vision for any person in this world. Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah differed on this into two schools. Among them are those who said that revelation only indicates vision in the Hereafter, but rather what revelation has brought is the impossibility of any person seeing his Lord before death, due to the raised hadith which Muslim narrated from 'Ubadah ibn al-Samit that he said: 'Know that you will not see your Lord until you die.' The leader of this opinion among the Companions is Lady 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her. Al-Bukhari and others narrated from Masruq that he said: I said to 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'O mother, did Muhammad see his Lord?' She said: 'My hair stood on end from what you said. Where are you from three things: whoever tells you that Muhammad saw his Lord has lied.' Then she recited: 'Vision does not perceive Him, but He perceives all vision, and He is the Subtle, the Aware' (al-An'am 103), and 'It is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil' (al-Shura 51). And whoever tells you that he knows what will be tomorrow has lied. Then she recited: 'No soul knows what it will earn tomorrow' (Luqman 34). And whoever tells you that he concealed has lied, and she recited: 'O Messenger, convey that which has been sent down to you' (al-Ma'idah 67). But he saw Jibril, peace be upon him, in his form twice. And the majority went that revelation has indicated the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in the abode of this world, and the leader of this opinion is 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, along with the majority of the Companions. Among their most important proofs on that is the hadith of al-Isra' and al-Mi'raj and the saying of Allah Most High: 'And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind' (al-Isra' 60), and many hadiths have been narrated in that regard. The first group denied the validity of using this verse as evidence, arguing that 'al-ru'ya' (with alif) is used for the dream vision, not reality. However, the response to that is that 'al-ru'ya', just as it is used for the dream vision, is also used for the waking vision without distinction. This is supported by the saying of the poet: 'He said 'Allahu Akbar' for the vision and his heart cheered, and gladdened a heart that had been full of calamities.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage discusses the third aspect of the vision of Allah: whether revelation indicates that it can occur or be possible in this world. The author states that Ahl al-Sunnah split into two opinions. The first opinion, led by 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her), holds that revelation only indicates vision in the Hereafter and actually negates it before death. This is based on the hadith 'You will not see your Lord until you die' (narrated by Muslim) and 'A'isha's own statements. 'A'isha vehemently denied that the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw his Lord in this life, citing verses that vision does not encompass Allah (al-An'am 103) and that Allah only speaks to humans through revelation or from behind a veil (al-Shura 51). She also denied any knowledge of the unseen or concealment of revelation. She affirmed that the Prophet only saw Jibril in his true form twice. The second opinion, led by 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him), is that revelation permits seeing Allah in this world. Their main evidence includes the verse in surah al-Isra' (17:60) about the vision shown to the Prophet during the Night Journey, along with many hadiths. The first group objected to this reasoning, arguing that 'al-ru'ya' (with a definite article and alif) refers to a dream vision, not a waking vision. The author responds by explaining that 'al-ru'ya' is used for both dream and waking visions without distinction, citing a line of poetry where the poet refers to a waking vision. Thus, the objection is refuted. The passage does not definitively settle the issue but presents the two views and the counter-argument.

Key Terms
ابن عباس
الرؤيا
الإسراء والمعراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the word 'ru'ya' only means dream and cannot refer to waking vision.
Correction: The author shows it is used for both, citing poetry.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author provide to refute the objection that 'al-ru'ya' only means dream vision?
A) A hadith
B) A verse from the Quran
C) A line of poetry
D) A statement of Ibn Abbas

Key Takeaway
The majority opinion, led by Ibn Abbas, supports the possibility of seeing Allah in this world, and the linguistic objection is countered by the broad usage of 'ru'ya'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Abbas and the majority believed seeing Allah in this world is possible. Does your heart truly long for that vision, or do you hide behind intellectual doubts? What would change in your worship if you lived with the hope of seeing your Lord today?
?
The poet’s heart was gladdened by a vision (al-ru'ya). When was the last time your heart was truly cheered by a sense of Allah’s presence? Could it be that you have closed yourself off from such experiences by assuming they are impossible? What step can you take this week to sincerely seek a vision of Allah in your life?
?
If the word 'ru'ya' can apply to waking visions, then the possibility of seeing Allah is real. Does this fill you with hope or fear? What does your reaction reveal about your relationship with your Lord?

Meaning of Belief in the Heavenly Books
Defines belief in heavenly books and clarifies its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of belief in the heavenly books and its implications regarding textual alteration and the applicability of their laws after Islam.

Core Idea
Belief in previous scriptures means affirming their divine origin, not preserving their current texts.

The author clarifies what it truly means to believe in the heavenly books. He explains that this belief does not require accepting current versions as unchanged, nor does it require following their laws after Islam. This passage dispels common misconceptions about the status of earlier scriptures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's precise language and the two 'لا يستلزم' phrases.
The passage is dense with important distinctions.
2
Identify the two negations
The author says belief does not necessitate two things. Find both negations (لا يستلزم) and note what they are.
The first negation concerns the current authenticity of the books, the second concerns the applicability of their laws.
3
Reflect on the example of Paul
Think about why the author chose the example of Paul. What does it illustrate about the nature of alteration?
This shows that distortion was deliberate and not just accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى الإيمان بها ، الاعتقاد بأنها وحي من الله عز وجل للأقوام الذين أرسل إليهم الرسل الذين بعثوا بها . وهذا لا يستلزم الاعتقاد بأن الكتب اليوم لا يزال حقاً من عند الله عز وجل ، بل الواقع اليقيني أن التبديل والتحريف قد شاع كل منهما في هذه الكتب ، مع تطاول الزمن ، وبفعل كثير ممن استغلوا دين الله عز وجل لأغراض كثيرة مختلفة ؛ وخير مثال تاريخي واضح في هذا الصدد، ما فعله بولس بالنسبة للإنجيل ، من تلاعب به وتغيير بحقائقه وإتلاف لما لم يعجبه منه وإقحام ما رأى إقحامه فيه بمقتضى رأيه الكاسد واختراعه الباطل
كما أن ضرورة الإيمان بهذه الكتب لا تستلزم ضرورة الإيمان بأن كل مافي مضمونها من الأحكام التشريعية يجب الأخذ به وتطبيقه بعد بعثة محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام .
And the meaning of belief in them is the conviction that they are revelation from Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, to the nations to whom the messengers who were sent with them were sent. And this does not necessitate the belief that the books today are still true from Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, but rather the certain reality is that alteration and distortion have become widespread in these books, both of them, with the passage of time, and by the action of many who exploited the religion of Allah for many different purposes; and a clear historical example in this regard is what Paul did with the Gospel: tampering with it, changing its truths, destroying what he did not like of it, and inserting what he saw fit to insert into it according to his corrupt opinion and false invention. Likewise, the necessity of belief in these books does not necessitate the necessity of belief that every legislative ruling in their content must be adopted and applied after the mission of Muhammad, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of belief in the heavenly books (al-kutub al-samawiyya). The author begins by stating that belief in them means believing that they were originally revelation (waḥy) from Allah to the nations to which the messengers were sent. This belief, however, does not require one to believe that the current versions of these books are still entirely authentic divine texts. Rather, the certain reality is that both alteration (tabdīl) and distortion (taḥrīf) have become widespread in these books over time, due to the actions of many who exploited the religion of Allah for various purposes. The author provides the historical example of Paul (Būlus) and his manipulation of the Gospel—tampering, changing truths, destroying what he disliked, and inserting his own corrupt opinions and false inventions. The author then clarifies that the obligation to believe in these books also does not require the obligation to believe that every legislative ruling in their content must be adopted and applied after the mission of Muhammad (peace be upon him). Thus, belief in the earlier scriptures is limited to affirming their original divine origin and their core message, not to accepting later alterations or applying their laws after Islam.

Key Terms
وحي
التبديل
التحريف
بولس
الإنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing in the heavenly books means accepting all their current content as unchanged and applicable today.
Correction: Belief in them is limited to affirming their original divine revelation; their current texts have been altered, and their laws are superseded by Islam.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the current state of previous heavenly books?
A) They are entirely preserved as revealed.
B) They have been altered and distorted over time.
C) They are unchanged but superseded.
D) They are still the only valid source of law.

Key Takeaway
Belief in previous scriptures affirms their original revelation but does not require belief in their current textual integrity or the applicability of their laws after Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever naively assumed that the Torah and Gospel in your local synagogue or church are exactly as revealed? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you do to root out any such false assumption from your heart?
?
If you were asked by a Jew or Christian about your belief in their scriptures, would you accurately explain that you affirm their divine origin but not their current integrity? If not, what holds you back from being clear about this distinction, and how can you prepare to give a truthful answer?
?
This lesson shows that even the original Gospel was tampered with by Paul. Do you have any cherished ideas about the 'Bible' that might be based on its current form rather than the original revelation? Are you willing to let go of those ideas in order to fully embrace the Islamic teaching? What concrete step will you take this week to examine your own beliefs about previous scriptures?

The Meaning of Belief in Heavenly Books
Summary of belief in heavenly books and their contents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in the heavenly books entails affirming their divine origin, their core message of monotheism, and that their legal rulings are subject to abrogation.

Core Idea
Heavenly books share the unchanging core of tawhid but their laws are abrogated.

In this lesson, we summarize the essential meaning of the obligation to believe in the heavenly books. The author explains that they are revealed from Allah, contain the eternal creed of monotheism, and include legislative rulings that are mostly abrogated by later revelations. This sets the stage for understanding the finality of Muhammad's prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first gives a general summary.
The passage is the conclusion of a larger discussion.
2
Identify the three elements of belief in books
The author mentions three things: they are revealed, they contain tawhid, and they contain laws that are mostly abrogated. List them.
The abrogation is by later books.
3
Understand the significance of 'most' of the rulings being abrogated
The author says 'most' not 'all', because some rulings may remain if not abrogated, but the eternal core is tawhid.
Tawhid remains unchanged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخلاصة أن معنى ضرورة الإيمان بالكتب السماوية أنها كتب موحى بها في الأصل من الله عز وجل ، وأنها تحتوي على عقيدة التوحيد الخالص الباقية مدى الدهر ، كما تحوي أحكاماً تشريعية - قلّت أو كثرت - ولكن معظمها منسوخ بما قد جاء بعدها . وتفصيل ذلك في بيان البند السادس والأخير :
1 - إن شريعة خاتم الأنبياء محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ناسخة لجميع الشرائع السماوية السابقة : والمقصود بالشرائع - كما تعلم - تلك الأحكام العملية المتعلقة بالعبادات أو المعاملات المختلفة . فلا يعد الإيمان بنبوة سيدنا محمد إيماناً كافياً حتى يضاف إليه الإيمان بأن ما بعث به من الشرع ناسخ لكل ما كان قبله من شرائع الأنبياء السابقين
And the summary is that the meaning of the necessity of belief in the heavenly books is that they are books originally revealed from Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, and that they contain the creed of pure monotheism, remaining forever, and they contain legislative rulings—few or many—but most of them are abrogated by what has come after them. And the detail of that is in the statement of the sixth and last point: 1. Indeed, the law of the Seal of the Prophets, Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is abrogative of all previous heavenly laws. And what is meant by laws (al-sharāʾiʿ)—as you know—are those practical rulings related to acts of worship or various transactions. So belief in the prophethood of our master Muhammad is not considered sufficient belief unless there is added to it the belief that what he was sent with of the law is abrogative of all that was before it of the laws of the previous prophets.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage provides a summary of the meaning of the obligation to believe in the heavenly books. The author states that these books are originally revealed from Allah, containing the pure creed of monotheism (ʿaqīdat al-tawḥīd al-khāliṣ) which remains forever, and they also contain legislative rulings (aḥkām tashrīʿiyya)—whether few or many—but most of these rulings are abrogated (mansūkh) by what has come after them. The author then introduces the sixth and final point, which is that the law of the Seal of the Prophets, Muhammad (peace be upon him), is abrogative (nāsikha) of all previous heavenly laws. He clarifies that by 'laws' (sharāʾiʿ) is meant the practical rulings related to acts of worship and transactions. He emphasizes that belief in Muhammad's prophethood is not sufficient unless accompanied by belief that the law he brought abrogates all previous laws of the prophets.

Key Terms
الشرائع السماوية
التوحيد
منسوخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that all contents of earlier heavenly books remain valid today.
Correction: The author clarifies that the core creed of tawhid remains, but most legislative rulings are abrogated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what remains unchanged in the heavenly books?
A) The legislative rulings.
B) The creed of pure monotheism.
C) The language.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Belief in heavenly books means affirming their divine origin, the eternal tawhid, and that their legal rulings are mostly abrogated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every previous scripture, including the Torah and Gospel, was originally revealed from Allah? If so, how does that shape your respect for those who follow them before the coming of the Quran? Are you willing to honor them as divine revelations while accepting the Quran as the final and most complete?
?
When you read about the abrogation of previous laws, does it settle in your heart that Allah's wisdom adapts His guidance to different times, or do you feel a sense of loss? Is there any part of you that wishes some of those earlier laws were still in practice, and how does this lesson correct that feeling?
?
The lesson says the core of tawḥīd in all books remains forever. Does your daily life reflect that you are committed to this pure monotheism as the unchanging foundation, or have you let distractions and innovations obscure it? What is one attachment in your life that you need to realign with the tawḥīd taught in every scripture?

Abrogation by Muhammad's Law
Statement of abrogation by Muhammad's law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Muhammad's law abrogates all previous heavenly laws and that belief in his prophethood requires belief in this abrogation.

Core Idea
Muhammad's law abrogates all prior laws; belief in him requires accepting that.

The author now turns to the critical sixth point: the law of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) abrogates all previous laws. He defines what is meant by 'laws' (sharāʾiʿ) and emphasizes that belief in Muhammad's prophethood is incomplete without acknowledging that his law supersedes all earlier ones. This lesson explains that core requirement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the sixth point
Focus on the Arabic text starting from 'وتفصيل ذلك...' and the translation. Note the strong statement.
The passage is from the original source.
2
Understand what 'sharāʾiʿ' means in this context
The author clarifies that it refers to practical rulings on worship and transactions, not creed.
Creed is eternal.
3
Grasp the condition for sufficient belief in Muhammad
The author states that belief in his prophethood is not sufficient unless it includes belief that his law abrogates all previous laws.
This is a necessary component of faith.
4
Reflect on why this matters
This establishes the finality and universality of Muhammad's message.
It also explains why Muslims follow Islamic law exclusively.

Buti's Evidence for Human Superiority
Buti cites Quran and hadith for human superiority over angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational arguments Buti presents for the superiority of believing humans over angels.

Core Idea
Humans earn reward by overcoming desires, which angels lack.

In this passage, Buti presents three lines of evidence to support the view that believing humans are superior to angels: a Quranic verse, a hadith, and a rational argument about the components of obligation. He shows that humans have a unique capacity to earn reward through struggle against their passions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic evidence
Buti cites the verse from Al-Bayyinah calling believers the best of creatures, noting that 'creatures' includes angels.
This verse is often used to argue for human superiority.
2
Read the hadith evidence
He cites the hadith that angels lower their wings for the seeker of knowledge, implying honor for humans.
This hadith is from Abu Dawud.
3
Understand the rational argument
Buti argues that humans are equipped with desires and passions, and by overcoming them they earn a reward that angels cannot earn because angels lack these desires.
This argument highlights the unique struggle of humans.
4
Reflect on the combined evidence
Consider how these three pieces together build a case for human superiority, but also prepare for Qurtubi's caution in the next lesson.

ذلك قول الله تعالى ﴿ إن الذينَ آمَنوا وعملوا الصَّالِحاتِ أُولئِكَ هُمْ خَيْرُ البَرِيَّة ) [ البينة : ٧] والبريّة تشمل الملائكة ، وبالحديث الذي أخرجه أبو داود وغيره عن رسول الله مع العلم أنه قال : « إن الملائكة لتضع أجنحتها رضاً لطالب العلم » كما استدلوا على ذلك بما جهز الله به الإنسان من مقومات التكليف التي بها يستأهل الأجر والمثوبة على القيام بما كلفه الله به من واجبات ، فقد ركب الله فيه مختلف الشهوات والأهواء التي يستأهل بمقارعتها والتغلب عليها أجراً لا يستأهله الملائكة ، لعدم وجود شيء من هذه الشهوات والأهواء في تركيبهم الوجودي )

قال القرطبي : ولا طريق إلى القطع بأن الأنبياء أفضل من الملائكة ، ولا القطع بأن الملائكة خير منهم ، لأن طريق ذلك خبر الله تعالى وخبر رسوله ( أي المتواتر من ذلك ( أو إجماع الأمة ، وليس هاهنا شيء منه
That is the saying of God Most High {Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds - those are the best of creatures} [Al-Bayyinah 7]. And 'creatures' includes the angels. And by the hadith narrated by Abu Dawud and others from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, that he said: 'Indeed the angels lower their wings out of pleasure for the seeker of knowledge.' And they also adduced as evidence what God has equipped man with of the components of obligation by which he deserves reward and recompense for performing the duties God has obligated him with. For God has placed in him various desires and passions, by resisting and overcoming which he deserves a reward that the angels do not deserve, due to the absence of any of these desires and passions in their existential constitution. Al-Qurtubi said: There is no way to be certain that the prophets are better than the angels, nor to be certain that the angels are better than them, because the path to that is the report of God Most High and the report of His Messenger (i.e., the mutawatir among that) or the consensus of the ummah, and nothing of that is present here.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents scriptural evidence for the superiority of humans over angels, followed by a cautious conclusion from Qurtubi. The author begins by citing the Quranic verse that calls believers the best of creatures, noting that 'creatures' includes angels. He then cites a hadith that angels lower their wings for knowledge seekers, implying honor for humans. Additionally, he argues that humans are equipped with desires and passions, and by overcoming them they earn a reward that angels cannot earn because angels lack these passions. This shows humans can achieve a higher level. Then he quotes Qurtubi, who states that there is no definitive proof from scripture that prophets are better than angels or vice versa, since decisive proof would require a mutawatir report or consensus, and neither exists. This introduces a note of caution: while some verses suggest human superiority, it is not cut-and-dry.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quranic verse alone definitively proves all believers are better than all angels in every way.
Correction: The verse indicates a general superiority but is not decisive on its own, as Qurtubi's caution shows. Moreover, the hadith and rational argument strengthen the case, but still not absolutely decisive.

Buti presents three compelling proofs that believing humans are superior to angels: a Quranic verse, a hadith, and a rational argument about struggling against desires.

?

?

?

Qurtubi's Caution on Decisive Proof
Qurtubi concludes no definitive proof exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Qurtubi's view that there is no certain proof from revelation or consensus to decide whether prophets are better than angels or vice versa.

Core Idea
Without mutawatir proof or consensus, the matter remains open.

After presenting evidence for human superiority, Buti quotes the caution of Imam al-Qurtubi. Qurtubi reminds us that decisive theological conclusions require either a mutawatir report or consensus of the ummah, and since neither exists on this topic, the question of whether prophets are better than angels cannot be settled with certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read Qurtubi's statement
Qurtubi says there is no way to be certain that prophets are better than angels or vice versa.
This is a cautious position from a renowned scholar.
2
Understand the criteria for decisive proof
Qurtubi explains that such certainty requires either a mutawatir report or consensus of the ummah.
Mutawatir means widely transmitted so that doubt is impossible.
3
Reflect on the absence of such proof
He states that nothing like that exists on this topic.
Thus the question remains open.
